The **United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland**, commonly known as the **United Kingdom** (**UK**) or **Britain**,[^12] is a country in [northwestern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northwestern_Europe "Northwestern Europe"), off the coast of [the continental mainland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_mainland "European mainland"). It comprises [England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/England "England"), [Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotland "Scotland"), [Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wales "Wales") and [Northern Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Ireland "Northern Ireland"),[^13] with a population of over 69 million in 2024. The UK includes the island of [Great Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Britain "Great Britain"), the north-eastern part of the island of [Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ireland "Ireland"), and most of [the smaller islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_islands_of_the_United_Kingdom "List of islands of the United Kingdom") within the [British Isles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Isles "British Isles"), covering 94,354 square miles (244,376 km <sup>2</sup>).[^6] It shares [a land border](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_border "Republic of Ireland–United Kingdom border") with the [Republic of Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland "Republic of Ireland") and is surrounded by the [Atlantic Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean "Atlantic Ocean"), the [North Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Sea "North Sea"), the [English Channel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_Channel "English Channel"), the [Celtic Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celtic_Sea "Celtic Sea") and the [Irish Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Sea "Irish Sea"), while maintaining sovereignty over the [Crown Dependencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_Dependencies "Crown Dependencies") and the [British Overseas Territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories "British Overseas Territories"). The capital and largest city of England and the UK is [London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/London "London"); [Edinburgh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edinburgh "Edinburgh"), [Cardiff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiff "Cardiff") and [Belfast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belfast "Belfast") are the national capitals of Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland, respectively.

The British Isles have been continuously inhabited since the [Neolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neolithic "Neolithic"). In AD 43 the [Roman conquest of Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain "Roman conquest of Britain") began. The [Roman departure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain "End of Roman rule in Britain") between 383 and 410 was followed by [Anglo-Saxon settlement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain "Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain") beginning around 450. In 1066 the [Normans conquered England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_Conquest "Norman Conquest"). Over the 17th century the role of the [British monarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_monarchy "British monarchy") was reduced, particularly as a result of the [English Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_Civil_War "English Civil War"). In 1707 the Kingdom of England and the [Kingdom of Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland "Kingdom of Scotland") united under the [Treaty of Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Union "Treaty of Union") to create the [Kingdom of Great Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain "Kingdom of Great Britain"). The [Acts of Union 1800](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800 "Acts of Union 1800") incorporated the [Kingdom of Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland "Kingdom of Ireland") to create the [United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland "United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland") in 1801. Most of Ireland [seceded](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland "Partition of Ireland") from the UK in 1922 as the [Irish Free State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Free_State "Irish Free State"), and the [Royal and Parliamentary Titles Act 1927](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_and_Parliamentary_Titles_Act_1927 "Royal and Parliamentary Titles Act 1927") created the present United Kingdom.

The UK [became the first industrialised country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_industrialisation#Industrial_Revolution_in_Europe "History of industrialisation") and was the world's [foremost power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foremost_power "Foremost power") for the majority of the 19th and early 20th centuries, particularly during the *[Pax Britannica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pax_Britannica "Pax Britannica")* between 1815 and 1914. The [British Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire") was the leading [economic power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_power "Economic power") for most of the 19th century, a position supported by [its agricultural prosperity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution "British Agricultural Revolution"), its role as a dominant [trading nation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trading_nation "Trading nation"), a massive industrial capacity, [significant technological achievements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_British_innovations_and_discoveries_in_the_19th_century "List of British innovations and discoveries in the 19th century"), and the rise of [19th-century London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/19th-century_London "19th-century London") as the world's principal financial centre. At its height in the 1920s, the empire encompassed around a quarter of the world's landmass and population, and was the [largest in history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_largest_empires#Empires_at_their_greatest_extent "List of largest empires"). However, [its involvement in the First World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War "History of the United Kingdom during the First World War") and [in the Second World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_World_War_II "Military history of the United Kingdom during World War II") damaged [Britain's economic power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_the_British_Empire "Economy of the British Empire"), and a global wave of [decolonisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decolonisation "Decolonisation") led to the independence of most British colonies.

The UK is a [constitutional monarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy "Constitutional monarchy") and [parliamentary democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy "Parliamentary democracy") [^14] with three distinct jurisdictions: [England and Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_law "English law"), [Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scots_law "Scots law"), and [Northern Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_Northern_Ireland "Law of Northern Ireland"). Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland have their own governments and parliaments which control various [devolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Devolution_in_the_United_Kingdom "Devolution in the United Kingdom") matters. A [developed country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developed_country "Developed country") with [an advanced economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_Kingdom "Economy of the United Kingdom"), the UK ranks amongst the [largest economies by nominal GDP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(nominal\) "List of countries by GDP (nominal)") and is one of the world's [largest exporters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_exports "List of countries by exports") and [importers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_imports "List of countries by imports"). As a [nuclear state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_state "Nuclear state") with one of the [highest defence budgets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures "List of countries by military expenditures"), the UK maintains [one of the strongest militaries in Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Armed_Forces "British Armed Forces"). [British culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom "Culture of the United Kingdom") is globally influential, notably in the [Anglosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglosphere "Anglosphere") and [the Commonwealth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations "Commonwealth of Nations"); [its soft power influence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglicisation "Anglicisation") is observable in the legal and political systems of many [former colonies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_that_have_gained_independence_from_the_United_Kingdom "List of countries that have gained independence from the United Kingdom"), and in its exports [of language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English-speaking_world "English-speaking world"), [literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_literature "British literature"), [theatre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theatre_of_the_United_Kingdom "Theatre of the United Kingdom"), [cinema](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_Kingdom "Cinema of the United Kingdom"), [music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music_of_the_United_Kingdom "Music of the United Kingdom"), [art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art_of_the_United_Kingdom "Art of the United Kingdom") and [sport](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sport_in_the_United_Kingdom "Sport in the United Kingdom"). A [great power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_power "Great power"), the UK is part of [numerous international organisations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_United_Kingdom#International_organisations "Foreign relations of the United Kingdom").

## Etymology and terminology

The [Acts of Union 1707](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707 "Acts of Union 1707") declared that the [Kingdom of England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_England "Kingdom of England") and the [Kingdom of Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland "Kingdom of Scotland") were "United into One Kingdom by the Name of Great Britain".[^15] [^41] The term "United Kingdom" has occasionally been used for the former [Kingdom of Great Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain "Kingdom of Great Britain"), although its official name from 1707 to 1800 was simply "Great Britain".[^42] The [Acts of Union 1800](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800 "Acts of Union 1800") formed the [United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland "United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland"). Following the [partition of Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland "Partition of Ireland") and the independence of the [Irish Free State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Free_State "Irish Free State") in 1922, which left [Northern Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Ireland "Northern Ireland") as the only part of the island of Ireland within the United Kingdom, the name was changed in 1927 to the "United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland".[^43]

Although the United Kingdom is a sovereign country, England, Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland are widely referred to as [countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom "Countries of the United Kingdom").[^44] The UK Prime Minister's website has used the phrase "countries within a country" to describe it.[^45] Some statistical summaries, such as those for the twelve [NUTS 1 regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NUTS_of_the_United_Kingdom "NUTS of the United Kingdom"), refer to Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland as "regions".[^46] Northern Ireland is also referred to as a "province".[^47] With regard to Northern Ireland, the descriptive name used "can be controversial, with the choice often revealing one's political preferences".[^48]

The term "Great Britain" conventionally refers to the island of Great Britain, or politically to England, Scotland and Wales in combination.[^49] It is sometimes used as a loose synonym for the United Kingdom as a whole.[^50] The word *England* is occasionally used incorrectly to refer to the United Kingdom as a whole, a mistake principally made by people from outside the UK.[^51]

The term "Britain" is used as a synonym for [Great Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Britain "Great Britain"),[^52] [^53] but also sometimes for the United Kingdom.[^54] [^53] Usage is mixed: the [style guide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Style_guide "Style guide") of the [UK Government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom "Government of the United Kingdom") prefers the term "UK" rather than "Britain" or "British" (except when referring to embassies [^16]),[^56] while other government documents acknowledge that both terms refer to the United Kingdom and that elsewhere "British government" is used at least as frequently as "United Kingdom government".[^57] The UK [Permanent Committee on Geographical Names](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permanent_Committee_on_Geographical_Names_for_British_Official_Use "Permanent Committee on Geographical Names for British Official Use") recognises "United Kingdom", "UK" and "U.K." as shortened and abbreviated geopolitical terms for the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland in its toponymic guidelines; it does not list "Britain" but notes that "it is only the one specific nominal term 'Great Britain' which invariably excludes Northern Ireland".[^57] The [BBC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC "BBC") historically preferred to use "Britain" as shorthand only for Great Britain, though the present style guide does not take a position except that "Great Britain" excludes Northern Ireland.[^58]

The adjective "British" is commonly used to refer to matters relating to the United Kingdom and is used in law to refer to United Kingdom citizenship and [nationality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_nationality_law "British nationality law").[^59] [^17] People of the United Kingdom use several different terms to describe their national identity and may identify themselves as being [British](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_people "British people"), [English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_people "English people"), [Scottish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_people "Scottish people"), [Welsh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Welsh_people "Welsh people"), [Northern Irish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People_of_Northern_Ireland "People of Northern Ireland") or [Irish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_people "Irish people");[^62] or as having a combination of different national identities.[^63]

## History

### Prior to the Treaty of Union

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg/250px-Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg)

Stonehenge in Wiltshire is a ring of stones, each about 4 m (13 ft) high, 2 m (7 ft) wide and 25 tonnes, erected 2400–2200 BC.

Settlement by [Cro-Magnons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cro-Magnons "Cro-Magnons") of what was to become the United Kingdom occurred in waves beginning by about 30,000 years ago.[^64] The island has been continuously inhabited since the last retreat of ice around 11,500 years ago.[^65] By the end of the [region's prehistoric period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain "Prehistoric Britain"), the population is thought to have belonged largely to a culture termed [Insular Celtic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insular_Celts "Insular Celts"), comprising [Brittonic Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Britons_\(historical\) "Britons (historical)") and [Gaelic Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland "Gaelic Ireland").[^66]

The [Roman conquest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain "Roman conquest of Britain"), beginning in AD 43, and the 400-year [rule of southern Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Britain "Roman Britain"), was followed by an invasion by [Germanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanic_peoples "Germanic peoples") [Anglo-Saxon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Saxon "Anglo-Saxon") settlers, reducing the Brittonic area mainly [to what was to become Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wales#Post-Roman_era "Wales"), [Cornwall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cornwall "Cornwall") and, until the latter stages of the Anglo-Saxon settlement, the [Hen Ogledd](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hen_Ogledd "Hen Ogledd") (northern England and parts of southern Scotland).[^67] Most of the [region settled by the Anglo-Saxons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England "Anglo-Saxon England") became unified as the Kingdom of England in the 10th century.[^68] [Gaelic speakers in north-west Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata "Dál Riata") (with connections to the north-east of Ireland and traditionally supposed to have migrated from there in the 5th century) [^69] united with the [Picts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Picts "Picts") to create the [Kingdom of Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland "Kingdom of Scotland") in the 9th century.[^70]

![Photograph of the Baths showing a rectangular area of greenish water surrounded by yellow stone buildings with pillars. In the background is the tower of the abbey.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg/250px-Roman_Baths_in_Bath_Spa%2C_England_-_July_2006.jpg)

The Roman Baths in Bath, Somerset, are a well-preserved thermae from Roman Britain.

In 1066 the [Normans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normans "Normans") invaded England from northern France. After [conquering England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England "Norman conquest of England") they seized [large parts of Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Wales "Norman invasion of Wales"), [conquered much of Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland "Norman invasion of Ireland") and were invited to settle in Scotland, bringing to each country [feudalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feudalism "Feudalism") on the Northern French model and [Norman-French](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman-French "Norman-French") culture.[^71] The [Anglo-Norman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Normans "Anglo-Normans") [ruling class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruling_class "Ruling class") greatly influenced, but eventually assimilated with, the local cultures.[^72] Subsequent [medieval English kings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet "House of Plantagenet") completed the [conquest of Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conquest_of_Wales "Conquest of Wales") and tried unsuccessfully [to annex Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence "Wars of Scottish Independence"). Asserting its independence in the 1320 [Declaration of Arbroath](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Declaration_of_Arbroath "Declaration of Arbroath"), Scotland maintained its independence thereafter, albeit in [near-constant conflict with England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Scottish_Wars "Anglo-Scottish Wars").[^73]

In 1215 [Magna Carta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magna_Carta "Magna Carta") was the first document to state that no government was above the law and that citizens have rights protecting them.[^74]

The English monarchs, through inheritance of [substantial territories in France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angevin_Empire "Angevin Empire") and claims to the French crown, were also heavily involved in conflicts in France, most notably the [Hundred Years' War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War "Hundred Years' War"), while the [Kings of Scots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Scottish_monarchs "List of Scottish monarchs") were in [an alliance with the French](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auld_Alliance "Auld Alliance") during this period.[^75] [Early modern Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_modern_Britain "Early modern Britain") saw religious conflict resulting from the [Reformation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reformation "Reformation") and the introduction of [Protestant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protestant "Protestant") state churches in each country.[^76] The [English Reformation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_Reformation "English Reformation") ushered in political, constitutional, social and cultural change in the 16th century and [established](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_religion "State religion") the [Church of England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_of_England "Church of England"). It defined a national identity for England and slowly, but profoundly, changed people's religious beliefs.[^77] Wales was [fully incorporated into the Kingdom of England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535_and_1542 "Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542"),[^78] and Ireland was constituted as a kingdom in personal union with the English crown.[^79] During the [Plantation of Ulster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster "Plantation of Ulster") in the 17th century, the lands of the independent Catholic Gaelic nobility were confiscated by the Crown and granted to Protestant settlers from England and Scotland.[^80]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg/250px-Bayeux_Tapestry_WillelmDux.jpg)

The Bayeux Tapestry depicts the Battle of Hastings, 1066, and the events leading to it.

The [Union of the Crowns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns "Union of the Crowns") occurred in 1603 when [James VI of Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland "James VI of Scotland") inherited the thrones of England and Ireland, subsequently moving his court from Edinburgh to London. Although unified under a single monarch, the three realms remained entirely separate political entities, preserving their respective parliaments, judicial systems, and established churches.[^81]

In the mid-17th century, all three kingdoms [were involved in a series of connected wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms "Wars of the Three Kingdoms") (including the [English Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_Civil_War "English Civil War")) which led to the temporary overthrow of the monarchy, with [the execution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Execution_of_Charles_I "Execution of Charles I") of [King Charles I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_I_of_England "Charles I of England"), and the establishment of the short-lived [unitary republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unitary_republic "Unitary republic") of the [Commonwealth of England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England "Commonwealth of England"), Scotland and Ireland.[^82]

Although the [monarchy was restored](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Restoration_\(1660\) "Restoration (1660)"), the [Interregnum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interregnum_\(1649%E2%80%931660\) "Interregnum (1649–1660)") along with the [Glorious Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glorious_Revolution "Glorious Revolution") of 1688 and the subsequent [Bill of Rights 1689](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689 "Bill of Rights 1689") in England and [Claim of Right Act 1689](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claim_of_Right_Act_1689 "Claim of Right Act 1689") in Scotland ensured that, unlike much of the rest of Europe, [royal absolutism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_absolutism "Royal absolutism") would not prevail, and a professed Catholic could never accede to the throne. The [British constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_constitution "British constitution") would develop on the basis of [constitutional monarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy "Constitutional monarchy") and the [parliamentary system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parliamentary_system "Parliamentary system").[^83] With the founding of the [Royal Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") in 1660, science was greatly encouraged. During this period, particularly in England, the development of [naval power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_navy "English navy") and the interest in [voyages of discovery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Discovery "Age of Discovery") led to the acquisition and settlement of overseas colonies, particularly in North America and the Caribbean.[^84]

Though previous attempts at uniting the two kingdoms within Great Britain in 1606, 1667 and 1689 had proved unsuccessful, the attempt initiated in 1705 led to the [Treaty of Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Union "Treaty of Union") of 1706 being agreed and ratified by both parliaments.

### Union of England and Scotland

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Articles_of_Union_between_England_and_Scotland_28_Jan_1707.png/250px-Articles_of_Union_between_England_and_Scotland_28_Jan_1707.png)

The Treaty of Union which unified the Kingdom of England and Kingdom of Scotland on 1 May 1707

On 1 May 1707 the Kingdom of Great Britain was formed, the result of the [Acts of Union 1707](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707 "Acts of Union 1707") between the [Kingdom of England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_England "Kingdom of England") and [Kingdom of Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland "Kingdom of Scotland").[^85] In the 18th century cabinet government developed under [Robert Walpole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Walpole "Robert Walpole"), who is considered the *[de facto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_facto "De facto")* first [prime minister](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom "Prime Minister of the United Kingdom") from 1721 to 1742. A series of [Jacobite uprisings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobite_uprisings "Jacobite uprisings") sought to remove the Protestant [House of Hanover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Hanover "House of Hanover") from the throne and restore the Catholic [House of Stuart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Stuart "House of Stuart"). In 1746, the Jacobites were defeated at the [Battle of Culloden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden "Battle of Culloden"), after which the [Scottish Highlanders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Highlanders "Scottish Highlanders") were forcibly assimilated into Scotland by revoking the feudal independence of [clan chiefs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_clan_chief "Scottish clan chief"). The British colonies in North America that broke away in the [American War of Independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War") became the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States"). British imperial ambition turned towards Asia, particularly to [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Raj "British Raj").[^86]

British merchants played a leading part in the [Atlantic slave trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade "Atlantic slave trade"), mainly between 1662 and 1807 when British or British-colonial [slave ships](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slave_ships "Slave ships") transported nearly 3.3 million slaves from Africa.[^87] The slaves were taken to [work on plantations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plantation_economy "Plantation economy"), principally [in the Caribbean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_British_West_Indies "History of the British West Indies") but also [in North America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_America "British America").[^88] However, with pressure from the [abolitionist movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_Kingdom "Abolitionism in the United Kingdom"), Parliament banned the trade in 1807, banned slavery in the British Empire in 1833, and Britain took a leading role in the movement to abolish slavery worldwide through the [blockade of Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blockade_of_Africa "Blockade of Africa") and pressing other nations to end their trade with a series of treaties.[^89]

### United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Victoria_in_her_Coronation_robes.jpg/250px-Victoria_in_her_Coronation_robes.jpg)

Victoria reigned as Queen of the United Kingdom and Empress of India during the 19th century.

In 1800, the parliaments of Great Britain and Ireland each passed an Act of Union, uniting the two kingdoms and creating the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland on 1 January 1801.[^90]

After the defeat of France at the end of the [French Revolutionary Wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars "French Revolutionary Wars") and [Napoleonic Wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars "Napoleonic Wars") (1792–1815), the United Kingdom emerged as the principal naval and imperial power (with London the [largest city in the world from about 1830](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_largest_cities_throughout_history "List of largest cities throughout history")).[^91] [Unchallenged at sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Royal_Navy_\(after_1707\)#Pax_Britannica,_1815%E2%80%931914 "History of the Royal Navy (after 1707)"), British dominance was later described as the *[Pax Britannica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pax_Britannica "Pax Britannica")* ("British Peace"), a period of [relative peace amongst the great powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_\(1814%E2%80%931919\) "International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)") (1815–1914) during which the British Empire became the global [hegemon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hegemon "Hegemon") [^92] [^93] and adopted the role of [global policeman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_policeman "Global policeman").[^94] [^95] From 1853 to 1856 Britain took part in the [Crimean War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crimean_War "Crimean War"), allied with the [Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire") against [Tsarist Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire").[^96] Following the [Indian Rebellion of 1857](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857 "Indian Rebellion of 1857") the British government led by [Lord Palmerston](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston "Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston") assumed [direct rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Direct_rule "Direct rule") over [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Raj "British Raj"). Alongside the formal control it exerted over its own colonies, British dominance of much of world trade meant that it [effectively controlled](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Informal_empire "Informal empire") the economies of regions such as [East Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asia "East Asia") and [Latin America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_America "Latin America").[^97]

Throughout the [Victorian era](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victorian_era "Victorian era") (1837–1901) political attitudes favoured [free trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_trade "Free trade") and *[laissez-faire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laissez-faire "Laissez-faire")* policies. Beginning with the [Great Reform Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Reform_Act "Great Reform Act") in 1832, Parliament gradually [widened the voting franchise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reform_Acts "Reform Acts"), with the [1884 Reform Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1884 "Representation of the People Act 1884") championed by [William Gladstone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone "William Ewart Gladstone") granting [suffrage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suffrage "Suffrage") to a majority of males for the first time. The British population increased at a dramatic rate, accompanied by rapid [urbanisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbanisation "Urbanisation"), causing significant social and economic stresses.[^98] By the late 19th century, [Conservative governments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservative_Party_\(UK\) "Conservative Party (UK)") under [Benjamin Disraeli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli "Benjamin Disraeli") and [Lord Salisbury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury "Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury") accelerated [imperial expansion in Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa "Scramble for Africa"), sustained a foreign policy of " [splendid isolation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Splendid_isolation "Splendid isolation") " from European alliances, and intensified strategic containment of the [Russian Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire") in [Central Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia"), a competition known as the [Great Game](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Game "Great Game").[^99] During this time, [Canada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada "Canada"), [Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia "Australia") and [New Zealand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand "New Zealand") were granted self-governing [dominion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dominion "Dominion") status.[^100] At the turn of the century, Britain's industrial dominance became challenged by the [German Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Empire "German Empire") and the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States").[^101] The [Edwardian era](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwardian_era "Edwardian era") (1901–1910) saw [social reform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberal_welfare_reforms "Liberal welfare reforms") and [home rule for Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_movement "Irish Home Rule movement") become important domestic issues, while the [Labour Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labour_Party_\(UK\) "Labour Party (UK)") emerged from an alliance of [trade unions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom "Trade unions in the United Kingdom") and small socialist groups in 1900, and [suffragettes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suffragettes "Suffragettes") campaigned for women's right to vote.[^102]

### World wars and partition of Ireland

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Wreaths_Are_Laid_at_the_Cenotaph%2C_London_During_Remembrance_Sunday_Service_MOD_45152052.jpg/250px-Wreaths_Are_Laid_at_the_Cenotaph%2C_London_During_Remembrance_Sunday_Service_MOD_45152052.jpg)

Wreaths being laid during the Remembrance Sunday service at the Cenotaph in Whitehall, London

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Spitfire_and_Hurricane_in_the_Battle_of_Britain_Memorial_Flight.jpg/250px-Spitfire_and_Hurricane_in_the_Battle_of_Britain_Memorial_Flight.jpg)

A Spitfire and a Hurricane as flown in the Battle of Britain during the Second World War

Britain was one of the principal [Allies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I "Allies of World War I") that defeated the [Central Powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Powers "Central Powers") in the [First World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_World_War "First World War") (1914–1918). Alongside their French, Russian and (after 1917) American counterparts,[^103] British armed forces were engaged across much of the British Empire and in several regions of Europe, particularly on the [Western Front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Front_\(World_War_I\) "Western Front (World War I)").[^104] The high fatalities of [trench warfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trench_warfare "Trench warfare") caused the loss of much of a generation of men, with lasting social effects in the nation and a great disruption in the social order. Britain had suffered 2.5 million casualties and finished the war with a huge national debt.[^104]

The consequences of the war persuaded the government to expand the right to vote in national and local elections to all adult men and most adult women with the [Representation of the People Act 1918](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918 "Representation of the People Act 1918").[^104] After the war Britain became a permanent member of the Executive Council of the [League of Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/League_of_Nations "League of Nations") and received [a mandate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate "League of Nations mandate") over a number of former German and [Ottoman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire") colonies. Under the leadership of [David Lloyd George](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Lloyd_George "David Lloyd George"), the British Empire reached its greatest extent, covering a fifth of the world's land surface and a quarter of its population.[^105]

By the mid-1920s most of the British population could listen to [BBC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC "BBC") radio programmes.[^106] [^107] Experimental [television](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Television_in_the_United_Kingdom "Television in the United Kingdom") broadcasts [began in 1929](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_television#United_Kingdom "History of television") and the [first scheduled BBC Television Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_day_of_BBC_television "First day of BBC television") commenced in 1936.[^108] The rise of [Irish nationalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_nationalism "Irish nationalism"), and disputes within Ireland over the terms of [Irish Home Rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule "Irish Home Rule"), led eventually to the [partition of the island](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland "Partition of Ireland") in 1921.[^109] [A period of conflict in what is now Northern Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Troubles_in_Ulster_\(1920%E2%80%931922\) "The Troubles in Ulster (1920–1922)") occurred from June 1920 until June 1922. The [Irish Free State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Free_State "Irish Free State") became independent, initially with [Dominion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dominion "Dominion") status in 1922, and [unambiguously independent in 1931](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1931#Irish_Free_State "Statute of Westminster 1931"). Northern Ireland remained part of the United Kingdom.[^110] The [1928 Equal Franchise Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1928_Equal_Franchise_Act "1928 Equal Franchise Act") gave women electoral equality with men in national elections. Strikes in the mid-1920s culminated in the [General Strike of 1926](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Strike_of_1926 "General Strike of 1926"). Still suffering from the long-term economic effects of the First World War, Britain was severely impacted by the [Great Depression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_Kingdom "Great Depression in the United Kingdom") (1929–1932), which intensified hardship in old industrial areas in traditional sectors, led to mass unemployment peaking near 3 million, and precipitated widespread political instability and social unrest. [A coalition government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Government_\(1931\) "National Government (1931)") was formed in 1931.[^111]

Nonetheless, Britain was described as "a very wealthy country, formidable in arms, ruthless in pursuit of its interests and sitting at the heart of a global production system." [^112] After [Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Germany "Nazi Germany") 's [invasion of Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland "Invasion of Poland") in 1939, Britain entered the [Second World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_World_War "Second World War"). Despite the defeat of its European allies in the first year, Britain and its empire continued the war against Germany.[^112]

In 1940 the [Royal Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Air_Force "Royal Air Force") defeated the German *[Luftwaffe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luftwaffe "Luftwaffe")* in the [Battle of Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Britain "Battle of Britain"). Urban areas suffered heavy bombing during [the Blitz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Blitz "The Blitz"). The [Grand Alliance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_Alliance_\(World_War_II\) "Grand Alliance (World War II)") of Britain, the United States and the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") formed in 1941, leading the [Allies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II "Allies of World War II") against the [Axis powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axis_powers "Axis powers"). There were eventual hard-fought victories in the [Battle of the Atlantic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic "Battle of the Atlantic"), the [North Africa campaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Africa_campaign "North Africa campaign") and the [Italian campaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_campaign_\(World_War_II\) "Italian campaign (World War II)"). British forces played important roles in the [Normandy landings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normandy_landings "Normandy landings") of 1944 and the [liberation of Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberation_of_Europe "Liberation of Europe"). The British Army led the [Burma campaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burma_campaign "Burma campaign") against Japan, and the [British Pacific Fleet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Pacific_Fleet "British Pacific Fleet") fought Japan at sea. [British scientists contributed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_contribution_to_the_Manhattan_Project "British contribution to the Manhattan Project") to the American [Manhattan Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manhattan_Project "Manhattan Project"), whose task was to build a [nuclear weapon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapon "Nuclear weapon").[^113]

### Post-war 20th century

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/500px-British_Empire_1921.png)

The British Empire at its territorial peak in 1921

The United Kingdom was one of the [Big Three](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Three_\(World_War_II\) "Big Three (World War II)") powers (with the United States and the Soviet Union) who met to plan the [post-war world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_II "Aftermath of World War II");[^114] it drafted the [Declaration by United Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Declaration_by_United_Nations "Declaration by United Nations") with the United States and became one of the five permanent members of the [United Nations Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council"). It worked closely with the United States to establish the [International Monetary Fund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund "International Monetary Fund"), the [World Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank") and [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO").[^115] The war left the UK severely weakened and financially dependent on the American [Marshall Plan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshall_Plan "Marshall Plan"),[^116] but it was spared the total war that devastated [eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe").[^117]

In the immediate post-war years the [Labour government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labour_Government_1945%E2%80%931951 "Labour Government 1945–1951") under [Clement Attlee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clement_Attlee "Clement Attlee") initiated a radical programme of reforms, which significantly affected British society [in the following decades](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-war_consensus "Post-war consensus").[^118] Major industries and public utilities were [nationalised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nationalised "Nationalised"), a [welfare state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Welfare_state "Welfare state") was established, and a comprehensive, publicly funded healthcare system, the [National Health Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Health_Service "National Health Service"), was created.[^119] The rise of nationalism in the colonies coincided with Britain's much-diminished economic position after [its involvement in the First World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War "History of the United Kingdom during the First World War") and [in the Second World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_World_War_II "Military history of the United Kingdom during World War II"), so that a policy of [decolonisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decolonisation "Decolonisation") was unavoidable.[^120] [^121] [^122] Independence was granted to India and Pakistan in 1947.[^123] Over the next three decades, most colonies of the British Empire gained their independence, and many became members of the [Commonwealth of Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations "Commonwealth of Nations").[^124]

The UK was the third country to develop [a nuclear weapons arsenal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom "Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom"), with its first atomic bomb test, [Operation Hurricane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Hurricane "Operation Hurricane"), in 1952, although the post-war limitations of Britain's international role were illustrated by the [Suez Crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis "Suez Crisis") of 1956. As a result of a shortage of workers in the 1950s, the government [encouraged immigration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom "Modern immigration to the United Kingdom") from [Commonwealth countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_countries "Commonwealth countries"). In the following decades the UK became a more [multiracial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiracialism "Multiracialism") and [multicultural](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiculturalism "Multiculturalism") society.[^125] Despite rising living standards in the late 1950s and 1960s, the United Kingdom's economic performance was less successful than many of its main competitors such as France, [West Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Germany "West Germany") and Japan. The United Kingdom was the first democratic nation to [lower its voting age to 18](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1969 "Representation of the People Act 1969") in 1969.[^126]

In the decades-long process of [European integration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_integration "European integration") the UK was a founding member of the [Western European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_European_Union "Western European Union"), established with the [London and Paris Conferences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/London_and_Paris_Conferences "London and Paris Conferences") in 1954. In 1960 the UK was one of the seven founding members of the [European Free Trade Association](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association "European Free Trade Association") (EFTA), but in 1973 it left to join the [European Communities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Communities "European Communities") (EC). In a [1975 referendum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1975_United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum "1975 United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum") 67 per cent voted to stay in it.[^127] When the EC became the [European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") (EU) in 1992, the UK was one of the 12 founding member states.

From the late 1960s, Northern Ireland experienced communal and [paramilitary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paramilitary "Paramilitary") violence, sometimes affecting other parts of the UK, known as [the Troubles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Troubles "The Troubles"). It is usually considered to have ended with the 1998 [Belfast "Good Friday" Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belfast_Agreement "Belfast Agreement").[^128] Following a period of widespread economic slowdown and industrial strife in the 1970s, the [Conservative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservative_Party_\(UK\) "Conservative Party (UK)") government of the 1980s led by [Margaret Thatcher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher "Margaret Thatcher") initiated a radical policy of [monetarism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monetarism "Monetarism"), [deregulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deregulation "Deregulation"), particularly of the financial sector (for example, the [Big Bang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang_\(financial_markets\) "Big Bang (financial markets)") in 1986) and labour markets, the sale of state-owned companies (privatisation), and the withdrawal of subsidies to others.[^129]

In 1982, [Argentina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina "Argentina") invaded the British territories of [South Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands "South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands") and the [Falkland Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falkland_Islands "Falkland Islands"), leading to the 10-week [Falklands War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falklands_War "Falklands War") in which Argentine forces were defeated. The inhabitants of the islands strongly favour British sovereignty, expressed in a [2013 referendum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2013_Falkland_Islands_sovereignty_referendum "2013 Falkland Islands sovereignty referendum"). From 1984 the British economy was helped by the inflow of substantial [North Sea oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Sea_oil "North Sea oil") revenues.[^130] Another British Overseas Territory, [Gibraltar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gibraltar "Gibraltar"),[^131] is [a key military base](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_of_Gibraltar "Port of Gibraltar"); a [referendum in 2002](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2002_Gibraltar_sovereignty_referendum "2002 Gibraltar sovereignty referendum") on shared sovereignty with Spain was rejected.

Around the end of the 20th century, major changes were made to the governance of the UK with the establishment of [devolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Devolution "Devolution") administrations for Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland.[^132] [The statutory incorporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998 "Human Rights Act 1998") followed acceptance of the [European Convention on Human Rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights "European Convention on Human Rights"). The UK remained a [great power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_modern_great_powers#United_Kingdom "List of modern great powers") with global diplomatic and military influence and a leading role in the United Nations and NATO.[^133]

### 21st century

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Johnson_signed_Brexit_Withdrawal_Agreement.jpg/250px-Johnson_signed_Brexit_Withdrawal_Agreement.jpg)

Prime Minister Boris Johnson signs the Brexit withdrawal agreement in 2020, formally withdrawing the UK from the European Union (EU).

The United Kingdom broadly supported the United States' approach to the " [war on terror](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_on_terror "War on terror") " in the early 21st century.[^134] British troops fought in the [war in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_\(2001%E2%80%932021\) "War in Afghanistan (2001–2021)"), but controversy surrounded Britain's [military deployment in Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq_War "Iraq War"), which saw the [largest protest in British history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/15_February_2003_anti-war_protests "15 February 2003 anti-war protests") in opposition to the government led by [Tony Blair](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tony_Blair "Tony Blair").[^135]

The [Great Recession](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Recession "Great Recession") (2007–2010) severely affected the British economy,[^136] and was followed by a period of weak growth and stagnation.[^137] [^138] The [Cameron–Clegg coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition "Cameron–Clegg coalition") government of 2010 introduced [austerity measures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom_government_austerity_programme "United Kingdom government austerity programme") intended to tackle the substantial public deficits.[^139] [A referendum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum "2014 Scottish independence referendum") on [Scottish independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_independence "Scottish independence") in 2014 resulted in the Scottish electorate voting by 55.3 to 44.7 per cent to remain part of the United Kingdom.[^140]

In 2016, 51.9 per cent of voters in the UK [voted to leave the European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2016_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum "2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum") (EU).[^141] The [UK left the EU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brexit "Brexit") in 2020.[^142] On 1 May 2021 the [EU–UK Trade and Cooperation Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EU%E2%80%93UK_Trade_and_Cooperation_Agreement "EU–UK Trade and Cooperation Agreement"), a free trade agreement between the UK and the EU, came into force.[^143] [^144]

The [COVID-19 pandemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_Kingdom "COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom") had a severe [impact on the British economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_impact_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_Kingdom "Economic impact of the COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom"), caused major [disruptions to education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic_on_education_in_the_United_Kingdom "Impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on education in the United Kingdom") and had [far-reaching impacts on society and politics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_impact_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_Kingdom "Social impact of the COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom") in 2020 and 2021.[^145] [^146] [^147] The United Kingdom was the first country in the world to use an approved [COVID-19 vaccine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/COVID-19_vaccine "COVID-19 vaccine"). Developing a [vaccine in the UK](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxford%E2%80%93AstraZeneca_COVID-19_vaccine "Oxford–AstraZeneca COVID-19 vaccine") allowed the rollout to be amongst the fastest in the world.[^148] [^149]

## Geography

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/MODIS_-_Great_Britain_and_Northern_Ireland_-_2012-06-04_during_heat_wave_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-MODIS_-_Great_Britain_and_Northern_Ireland_-_2012-06-04_during_heat_wave_%28cropped%29.jpg)

A satellite image of the United Kingdom excluding Shetland

The total area of the United Kingdom is approximately 94,354 square miles (244,376 km <sup>2</sup>),[^6] [^31] with a land area of 93,723 square miles (242,741 km <sup>2</sup>).[^31] It occupies the major part of the [British Isles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Isles "British Isles") [^150] and includes the island of Great Britain, the north-eastern one-sixth of the island of Ireland and [some smaller surrounding islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_islands_of_the_United_Kingdom "List of islands of the United Kingdom"), meaning it comprises [England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/England "England"), [Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotland "Scotland"), [Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wales "Wales") and [Northern Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Ireland "Northern Ireland").[^151]

Geographically, the United Kingdom lies between the [North Atlantic Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Atlantic_Ocean "North Atlantic Ocean") and the [North Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Sea "North Sea") with the southeast coast coming within 22 miles (35 km) of the coast of northern France, from which it is separated by the [English Channel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_Channel "English Channel").[^152]

The nearby island polities of the [Isle of Man](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Man "Isle of Man"), [Jersey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jersey "Jersey") and [Guernsey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guernsey "Guernsey") are [Crown Dependencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_Dependencies "Crown Dependencies"), in union with the [British monarch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom "Monarchy of the United Kingdom"), but do not strictly form part of the United Kingdom or any of its three jurisdictions or four countries, although the British government retains responsibility for their external affairs. The Isle of Man lies roughly midway between Great Britain and the [island of Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ireland "Ireland") in the [Irish Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Sea "Irish Sea"), while the Channel Islands lie just off the northern French coast.

The [Royal Greenwich Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich "Royal Observatory, Greenwich") in London was chosen as the defining point of the [Prime Meridian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Meridian "Prime Meridian") [^153] at the [International Meridian Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Meridian_Conference "International Meridian Conference") in 1884.[^154]

The UK lies between latitudes [49°](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/49th_parallel_north "49th parallel north") and [61° N](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/61st_parallel_north "61st parallel north"), and longitudes [9° W](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/9th_meridian_west "9th meridian west") and [2° E](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2nd_meridian_east "2nd meridian east"). Northern Ireland shares a 310-mile (499 km) land boundary with the Republic of Ireland [^152] and has a 404-mile (650 km) coastline.[^155] The length of coastline of Great Britain plus its principal islands is about 19,491 miles (31,368 km) long, with the coastline of the main island Great Britain being 11,073 miles (17,820 km) of that,[^156] though measurements can vary greatly due to the [coastline paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coastline_paradox "Coastline paradox").[^157] It is connected to continental Europe by the [Channel Tunnel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Channel_Tunnel "Channel Tunnel"), which at 31 miles (50 km) (24 miles (38 km) underwater) is the longest underwater tunnel in the world.[^158]

The UK contains four terrestrial [ecoregions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecoregion "Ecoregion"): [Celtic broadleaf forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celtic_broadleaf_forests "Celtic broadleaf forests"), [English Lowlands beech forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_Lowlands_beech_forests "English Lowlands beech forests"), [North Atlantic moist mixed forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Atlantic_moist_mixed_forests "North Atlantic moist mixed forests"), and [Caledonian conifer forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caledonian_Forest "Caledonian Forest").[^159] The area of woodland in the UK was estimated to be 3.25 million hectares in 2023, which represents 13 per cent of its land area.[^160]

### Climate

Most of the United Kingdom has a temperate climate, with generally cool temperatures and plentiful rainfall all year round.[^152] The temperature varies with the seasons seldom dropping below 0 [°C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celsius "Celsius") (32 [°F](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fahrenheit "Fahrenheit")) or rising above 30 °C (86 °F).[^161] Some parts, away from the coast, of upland England, Wales, Northern Ireland and most of Scotland, experience a [subpolar oceanic climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subpolar_oceanic_climate "Subpolar oceanic climate"). Higher elevations in Scotland experience a [continental subarctic climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subarctic_climate "Subarctic climate") and the mountains experience a [tundra climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tundra_climate "Tundra climate").[^162]

The prevailing wind is from the southwest and bears frequent spells of mild and wet weather from the Atlantic Ocean,[^152] although the eastern parts are mostly sheltered from this wind. Since the majority of the rain falls over the western regions, the eastern parts are the driest. Atlantic currents, warmed by the [Gulf Stream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_Stream "Gulf Stream"), bring mild winters, especially in the west where winters are wet and even more so over high ground. Summers are warmest in the southeast of England and coolest in the north. Heavy snowfall can occur in winter and early spring on high ground, and occasionally settles to great depth away from the hills.[^163]

The average total annual sunshine in the United Kingdom was 1,339.7 hours between 1971 and 2000.[^164] The hours of sunshine vary from 1,200 to about 1,580 hours per year.[^165]

Climate change has serious impacts on the country. A third of food price rise in 2023 was attributed to it.[^166] In 2024 the United Kingdom ranked 5th out of 180 countries in the [Environmental Performance Index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_Performance_Index "Environmental Performance Index").[^167] A law has been passed that [UK greenhouse gas emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_the_United_Kingdom "Greenhouse gas emissions by the United Kingdom") will be [net zero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_zero "Net zero") by 2050.[^168]

### Topography

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Uk_topo_en.jpg/250px-Uk_topo_en.jpg)

The United Kingdom's topography

[England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography_of_England "Geography of England") accounts for 53 per cent of the UK, covering 50,350 square miles (130,395 km <sup>2</sup>).[^169] Most of the country consists of lowland terrain,[^170] with upland and mountainous terrain northwest of the [Tees–Exe line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tees%E2%80%93Exe_line "Tees–Exe line") which roughly divides the UK into [lowland and upland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lowland_and_upland "Lowland and upland") areas. Lowland areas include [Cornwall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cornwall "Cornwall"), the [New Forest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Forest "New Forest"), the [South Downs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Downs "South Downs") and the [Norfolk Broads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Broads "The Broads"). Upland areas include the [Lake District](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_District "Lake District"), the [Pennines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pennines "Pennines"), the [Yorkshire Dales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yorkshire_Dales "Yorkshire Dales"), [Exmoor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exmoor "Exmoor") and [Dartmoor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dartmoor "Dartmoor"). The main rivers and estuaries are the [Thames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River_Thames "River Thames"), [Severn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River_Severn "River Severn"), and the [Humber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humber "Humber"). England's highest mountain is [Scafell Pike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scafell_Pike "Scafell Pike"), at 978 metres (3,209 ft) in the Lake District; its largest island is the [Isle of Wight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Wight "Isle of Wight").

[Scotland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography_of_Scotland "Geography of Scotland") accounts for 32 per cent of the UK, covering 30,410 square miles (78,772 km <sup>2</sup>).[^171] This includes [nearly 800 islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Scotland "List of islands of Scotland"),[^172] notably the [Hebrides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrides "Hebrides"), [Orkney](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orkney "Orkney") Islands and [Shetland Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shetland_Islands "Shetland Islands"). Scotland is the most mountainous constituent country of the UK. The [Highlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Highlands "Scottish Highlands") to the north and west are the more rugged region containing the majority of Scotland's mountainous land, including the [Cairngorms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairngorms "Cairngorms"), [Loch Lomond and The Trossachs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loch_Lomond_and_The_Trossachs "Loch Lomond and The Trossachs") and [Ben Nevis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ben_Nevis "Ben Nevis") which at 1,345 metres (4,413 ft) [^173] is the highest point in the British Isles.[^174]

[Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography_of_Wales "Geography of Wales") accounts for less than 9 per cent of the UK, covering 8,020 square miles (20,779 km <sup>2</sup>).[^175] It is mostly mountainous, though [South Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Wales "South Wales") is less mountainous than [North](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Wales "North Wales") and [Mid Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mid_Wales "Mid Wales"). The highest mountains in Wales are in [Snowdonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snowdonia "Snowdonia") and include [Snowdon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snowdon "Snowdon") ([Welsh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Welsh_language "Welsh language"): *Yr Wyddfa*) which, at 1,085 metres (3,560 ft), is the highest peak in Wales.[^170] Wales has over 1,680 miles (2,704 kilometres) of coastline including the [Pembrokeshire Coast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pembrokeshire_Coast "Pembrokeshire Coast").[^156] Several islands lie off the Welsh mainland, the largest of which is [Anglesey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglesey "Anglesey") (*Ynys Môn*).

[Northern Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography_of_Ireland "Geography of Ireland"), separated from Great Britain by the [Irish Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_Sea "Irish Sea") and [North Channel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Channel_\(Great_Britain_and_Ireland\) "North Channel (Great Britain and Ireland)"), has an area of 5,470 square miles (14,160 km <sup>2</sup>) and is mostly hilly. It includes [Lough Neagh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lough_Neagh "Lough Neagh") which, at 150 square miles (388 km <sup>2</sup>), is the largest lake in the British Isles by area,[^176] [Lough Erne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lough_Erne "Lough Erne"), which has over 150 islands, and the [Giant's Causeway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giant%27s_Causeway "Giant's Causeway"), which is listed by [UNESCO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO") as a [World Heritage Site](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Heritage_Site "World Heritage Site"). The highest peak in Northern Ireland is [Slieve Donard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slieve_Donard "Slieve Donard") in the [Mourne Mountains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mourne_Mountains "Mourne Mountains") at 852 metres (2,795 ft).[^170]

[^1]: "God Save the King" is the [national anthem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_anthem "National anthem") by custom, not statute, and there is no authorised version. Typically only the first verse is usually sung, although the second verse is also often sung as well at state and public events.[^23] The words *King, he, him, his*, used at present, are replaced by *Queen, she, her* when the monarch is female.

[^2]: Scots, Ulster Scots, Welsh, Cornish, Scottish Gaelic and Irish are classed as [regional](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regional_language "Regional language") or [minority](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minority_language "Minority language") languages under the [Council of Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Council_of_Europe "Council of Europe") 's [European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages "European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages").[^24] These include defined obligations to promote those languages.[^25] See also [Languages of the United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom "Languages of the United Kingdom"). Welsh has limited *[de jure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_jure "De jure")* official status in Wales, as well as in the provision of national government services provided for Wales.

[^3]: Whereas the censuses of England and Wales and of Northern Ireland went ahead as planned in 2021, the census in Scotland was postponed until 2022. The Office for National Statistics reports that these UK-wide statistics "have been created to provide an image of how UK census data may have looked if the most recent UK censuses had been conducted with an aligned date of 21 March 2021".

[^4]: Although the United Kingdom has traditionally been seen as a [unitary state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unitary_state "Unitary state"), an alternative description of the UK as a "union state", put forward by, amongst others, [Vernon Bogdanor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vernon_Bogdanor "Vernon Bogdanor"),[^28] has become increasingly influential since the adoption of devolution in the 1990s.[^29] A union state is considered to differ from a unitary state in that while it maintains a central authority it also recognises the authority of historic rights and infrastructures of its component parts.[^30]

[^5]: [ONS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics "Office for National Statistics") Standard Area Measurement, "area to mean high water excluding inland water". Excludes the [Crown Dependencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_Dependencies "Crown Dependencies") and [British Overseas Territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories "British Overseas Territories").

[^6]: [ONS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics "Office for National Statistics") Standard Area Measurement, 'area to mean high water'. Excludes the [Crown Dependencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_Dependencies "Crown Dependencies") and [British Overseas Territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories "British Overseas Territories").

[^7]: Some of the devolved countries, Crown Dependencies and British Overseas Territories issue their own sterling banknotes or currencies, or use another nation's currency. See [List of British currencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_British_currencies "List of British currencies").

[^8]: Also observed by the [Crown Dependencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_Dependencies "Crown Dependencies"). For further information, see [Time in the United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time_in_the_United_Kingdom "Time in the United Kingdom").

[^9]: The UK Government uses the [ISO 8601](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISO_8601 "ISO 8601") format, yyyy - mm - dd, for machine-readable dates and times.[^36] See [Date and time notation in the United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Date_and_time_notation_in_the_United_Kingdom "Date and time notation in the United Kingdom").

[^10]: Excludes most overseas territories. See [Telephone numbers in the United Kingdom#Telephone numbers in Overseas Territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telephone_numbers_in_the_United_Kingdom#Telephone_numbers_in_Overseas_Territories "Telephone numbers in the United Kingdom").

[^11]: The [.gb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.gb ".gb") domain is also reserved for the UK, but has been little used.

[^12]: Usage is mixed. *[The Guardian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Guardian "The Guardian")* [^37] and *[The Telegraph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph "The Daily Telegraph")* [^38] use *Britain* as a synonym for the United Kingdom. The British [Cabinet Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cabinet_Office "Cabinet Office") 's [Government Digital Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_Digital_Service "Government Digital Service") style guide [^39] for use on [gov.uk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gov.uk "Gov.uk") recommends: "Use UK and United Kingdom in preference to Britain and British (UK business, UK foreign policy, ambassador and high commissioner). But British embassy, not UK embassy."

[^13]: The [Isle of Man](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Man "Isle of Man"), [Guernsey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bailiwick_of_Guernsey "Bailiwick of Guernsey") and [Jersey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jersey "Jersey") are [Crown Dependencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_Dependencies "Crown Dependencies") and not part of the UK.

[^14]: The United Kingdom does not have a codified constitution but an unwritten one formed of Acts of Parliament, court judgments, traditions, and conventions.[^40]

[^15]: Compare to section 1 of both of the 1800 [Acts of Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_Union_\(1800\) "Acts of Union (1800)") which reads: "the kingdoms of Great Britain and Ireland shall... be united into one kingdom, by the name of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland".

[^16]: "UK" embassies became "British" embassies in 1961.[^55]

[^17]: Historically, the term *British* was used to refer to members and institutions within the British Empire and later Commonwealth and was not limited to the geographical British Isles. The UK Government adopted the term for its exclusive use only in 1961, but in recognition of its wider usage first sought the prior consent of Australia, Canada and New Zealand.[^60] [^61]

[^18]: British [sovereignty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereignty "Sovereignty") derives from the Crown, a [corporation sole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corporation_sole "Corporation sole") occupied by the monarch. It is therefore by and through the monarch that Parliament exercises supreme legislative authority over both the executive and judiciary. Distinguished Professor of [Public Law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_Law "Public Law") Maurice Sunkin opined the Crown symbolically occupies "...what in other places would be a core element of a written constitution." [^182] As a result of this state of constitutional affairs, the monarch is formally referred to as " [the Sovereign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereign "Sovereign") " in legislation.[^183]

[^19]: For instance, the monarch alone appoints the prime minister and confers [state honours](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orders,_decorations,_and_medals_of_the_United_Kingdom "Orders, decorations, and medals of the United Kingdom") in the personal gift of the Crown. When necessary, the monarch may also refuse a [dissolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_Parliament "Dissolution of Parliament") or [prorogation of Parliament](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prorogation_in_the_United_Kingdom "Prorogation in the United Kingdom"), withhold [royal assent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_assent "Royal assent") to [primary legislation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Act_of_Parliament_\(UK\) "Act of Parliament (UK)"), and prevent illegal use of the [British Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Armed_Forces "British Armed Forces"), amongst other reserve powers.[^190]

[^20]: Car brands here are classed as British based on several of the following criteria: historical heritage, cultural significance, design and engineering base, manufacturing location, headquarters location, UK registered company (even with overseas investors).

[^21]: Berkeley is in fact Irish but was called a 'British empiricist' due to the territory of what is now known as the [Republic of Ireland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland "Republic of Ireland") being in the UK at the time.

[^22]: In 2012, the president of the IOC, [Jacques Rogge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacques_Rogge "Jacques Rogge"), stated, "This great, sports-loving country is widely recognised as the birthplace of modern sport. It was here that the concepts of sportsmanship and fair play were first codified into clear rules and regulations. It was here that sport was included as an educational tool in the school curriculum." [^543] [^544]

[^220]: Burrows, N. (1999). "Unfinished Business: The Scotland Act 1998". *The Modern Law Review*. **62** (2): 241–260 \[p. 249\]. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_\(identifier\) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1111/1468-2230.00203](https://doi.org/10.1111%2F1468-2230.00203). The UK Parliament is sovereign and the Scottish Parliament is subordinate. The White Paper had indicated that this was to be the approach taken in the legislation. The Scottish Parliament is not to be seen as a reflection of the settled will of the people of Scotland or of popular sovereignty but as a reflection of its subordination to a higher legal authority. Following the logic of this argument, the power of the Scottish Parliament to legislate can be withdrawn or overridden...; Elliot, M. (2004). ["United Kingdom: Parliamentary sovereignty under pressure"](https://doi.org/10.1093%2Ficon%2F2.3.545). *International Journal of Constitutional Law*. **2** (3): 545–627, 553–554. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_\(identifier\) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1093/icon/2.3.545](https://doi.org/10.1093%2Ficon%2F2.3.545). Notwithstanding substantial differences among the schemes, an important common factor is that the UK Parliament has not renounced legislative sovereignty in relation to the three nations concerned. For example, the Scottish Parliament is empowered to enact primary legislation on all matters, save those in relation to which competence is explicitly denied... but this power to legislate on what may be termed "devolved matters" is concurrent with the Westminster Parliament's general power to legislate for Scotland on any matter at all, including devolved matters... In theory, therefore, Westminster may legislate on Scottish devolved matters whenever it chooses...
